       <tr> 
        <td colspan="2" class="noprint" style="text-align:center;background:#ff99cc"> 
         <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"> 
          <ul> 
           <li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_thorium" title="Template:Infobox thorium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li> 
           <li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_thorium" title="Template talk:Infobox thorium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li> 
           <li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_thorium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li> 
          </ul> 
         </div> · <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td> 
       </tr> 
      </tbody>
     </table> 
     <p><b>Thorium</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Th</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 90. A <a href="/wiki/Radioactivity" title="Radioactivity" class="mw-redirect">radioactive</a> <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinide</a> <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a>, thorium is one of only two significantly radioactive elements that still occur naturally in large quantities as a <a href="/wiki/Primordial_element" title="Primordial element" class="mw-redirect">primordial element</a> (the other being <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>a<span>]</span></a></sup> It was discovered in 1828 by the Norwegian priest and amateur mineralogist <a href="/wiki/Morten_Thrane_Esmark" title="Morten Thrane Esmark">Morten Thrane Esmark</a><sup id="cite_ref-Krebs2006_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krebs2006-5"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> and identified by the Swedish chemist <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius" title="Jöns Jacob Berzelius">Jöns Jacob Berzelius</a>, who named it after <a href="/wiki/Thor" title="Thor">Thor</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Norse_god" title="Norse god" class="mw-redirect">Norse god</a> of thunder.</p> 
     <p>A thorium atom has 90 <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> and therefore 90 <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a>, of which four are <a href="/wiki/Valence_electron" title="Valence electron">valence electrons</a>. Thorium metal is silvery and <a href="/wiki/Tarnish" title="Tarnish">tarnishes</a> black when exposed to air. Thorium is weakly radioactive: all its known <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a> are unstable, with the seven naturally occurring ones (thorium-227, 228, 229, 230, 231, 232, and 234) having <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-lives</a> between 25.52&nbsp;hours and 14.05&nbsp;billion&nbsp;years. Thorium-232, which has 142 <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>, is the most stable isotope of thorium and accounts for nearly all natural thorium, with the other five natural isotopes occurring only in <a href="/wiki/Trace_radioisotope" title="Trace radioisotope">traces</a>: it decays very slowly through <a href="/wiki/Alpha_decay" title="Alpha decay">alpha decay</a> to <a href="/wiki/Radium-228" title="Radium-228" class="mw-redirect">radium-228</a>, starting a <a href="/wiki/Decay_chain" title="Decay chain">decay chain</a> named the <a href="/wiki/Thorium_series" title="Thorium series" class="mw-redirect">thorium series</a> that ends at <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>-208. Thorium is estimated to be about three to four times more abundant than <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> in the Earth's crust, and is chiefly refined from <a href="/wiki/Monazite" title="Monazite">monazite</a> sands as a by-product of extracting <a href="/wiki/Rare_earth_metal" title="Rare earth metal" class="mw-redirect">rare earth metals</a>.</p> 
     <p>Thorium was once commonly used as the light source in <a href="/wiki/Gas_mantle" title="Gas mantle">gas mantles</a> and as an alloying material, but these applications have declined due to concerns about its radioactivity. Thorium is still widely used as an alloying element in <a href="/wiki/TIG_welding" title="TIG welding" class="mw-redirect">TIG welding</a> electrodes (at a rate of 1%-2% mix with tungsten).<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> It remains popular as a material in high-end optics and scientific instrumentation; thorium and uranium are the only significantly radioactive elements with major commercial applications that do not rely on their radioactivity. Thorium is predicted to be able to replace uranium as nuclear fuel in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a>, but only a few <a href="/wiki/Thorium_fuel_cycle#Reactors" title="Thorium fuel cycle">thorium reactors</a> have yet been completed.</p> 
     <p></p>

       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li> 
      </ul> 
     </div> 
     <p></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Decay_Chain_Thorium.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Decay_Chain_Thorium.svg/300px-Decay_Chain_Thorium.svg.png" width="300" height="463" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Decay_Chain_Thorium.svg/450px-Decay_Chain_Thorium.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Decay_Chain_Thorium.svg/600px-Decay_Chain_Thorium.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1310" data-file-height="2020"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Decay_Chain_Thorium.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> The 4
        <i>n</i> 
        <a href="/wiki/Decay_chain" title="Decay chain">decay chain</a> of thorium-232, commonly called the "thorium series"
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical">Physical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Physical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Thorium is a <a href="/wiki/Hardness_(materials_science)" title="Hardness (materials science)" class="mw-redirect">soft</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paramagnetism" title="Paramagnetism">paramagnetic</a>, bright silvery radioactive actinide metal. In the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>, it is located to the right of the actinide <a href="/wiki/Actinium" title="Actinium">actinium</a>, to the left of the actinide <a href="/wiki/Protactinium" title="Protactinium">protactinium</a> and below the <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanide</a> <a href="/wiki/Cerium" title="Cerium">cerium</a>. Pure thorium is soft, very <a href="/wiki/Ductility" title="Ductility">ductile</a>, and can be <a href="/wiki/Cold-rolling#Cold_rolling" title="Cold-rolling" class="mw-redirect">cold-rolled</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swaging" title="Swaging">swaged</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Drawing_(manufacturing)" title="Drawing (manufacturing)">drawn</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The measured properties of thorium vary widely depending on the amount of impurities in the sample used: the major impurity is usually <a href="/wiki/Thorium_dioxide" title="Thorium dioxide">thorium dioxide</a> (ThO<sub>2</sub>). The purest thorium specimens usually contain about a tenth of a <a href="/wiki/Percent" title="Percent" class="mw-redirect">percent</a> of the dioxide.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Its density has been calculated to be 11.724&nbsp;g/cm<sup>3</sup>, while experimental measurements give values between 11.5&nbsp;and&nbsp;11.66&nbsp;g/cm<sup>3</sup>:<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> these values lie intermediate between those of its neighbours actinium (10.07&nbsp;g/cm<sup>3</sup>) and protactinium (15.37&nbsp;g/cm<sup>3</sup>), showing the continuity of trends across the actinide series.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> However, thorium's melting point of 1750&nbsp;°C is above both that of actinium (1227&nbsp;°C) and that of protactinium (1562±15&nbsp;°C): the melting points of the actinides do not have a clear dependence on their number of <a href="/wiki/Electron_configuration" title="Electron configuration">f electrons</a>, although there is a smooth trend downward from thorium to <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> where the number of f electrons increases from zero to six.<sup id="cite_ref-Yu._D._Tretyakov_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yu._D._Tretyakov-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> Thorium is a soft metal, having a bulk modulus of 54&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Gigapascal" title="Gigapascal" class="mw-redirect">GPa</a>, comparable to those of <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scandium" title="Scandium">scandium</a>. The hardness of thorium is similar to that of soft <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a>, so heated pure thorium can be rolled in sheets and pulled into wire.<sup id="cite_ref-Yu._D._Tretyakov_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yu._D._Tretyakov-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> Thorium becomes <a href="/wiki/Superconductor" title="Superconductor" class="mw-redirect">superconductive</a> below 1.40&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>b<span>]</span></a></sup> Nevertheless, while thorium is nearly half as dense as <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>, it is harder than either of them.<sup id="cite_ref-Yu._D._Tretyakov_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yu._D._Tretyakov-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> Among the actinides, thorium has the highest melting and boiling points and second-lowest density (second only to actinium).<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_7-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> The thermal expansion, electrical and thermal conductivities of thorium, protactinium, and uranium are comparable and are typical of <a href="/wiki/Post-transition_metal" title="Post-transition metal">post-transition metals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Thorium can also form <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a> with many other metals. With <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>, it forms <a href="/wiki/Eutectic_mixture" title="Eutectic mixture" class="mw-redirect">eutectic mixtures</a>, and thorium is completely <a href="/wiki/Miscibility" title="Miscibility">miscible</a> in both solid and liquid <a href="/wiki/State_of_matter" title="State of matter">states</a> with its lighter <a href="/wiki/Congener_(chemistry)" title="Congener (chemistry)">congener</a> cerium.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_7-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <table class="wikitable" style="margin:auto; text-align:center;">

     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical">Chemical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Chemical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Thorium is a highly <a href="/wiki/Reactivity_(chemistry)" title="Reactivity (chemistry)">reactive</a> metal. At <a href="/wiki/Standard_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard temperature and pressure" class="mw-redirect">standard temperature and pressure</a>, thorium is slowly attacked by water, but does not readily dissolve in most common acids, the exception being <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_7-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-11"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> It dissolves in concentrated <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a> containing a small amount of catalytic <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">fluoride</a> or <a href="/wiki/Fluorosilicate" title="Fluorosilicate" class="mw-redirect">fluorosilicate</a> ions;<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_7-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ekhyde_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ekhyde-12"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> if these are not present, <a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">passivation</a> can occur.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_7-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> At high temperatures, it is easily attacked by <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogens</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>. It can also form <a href="/wiki/Binary_compound" title="Binary compound">binary compounds</a> with <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_7-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> When thorium dissolves in hydrochloric acid, a black residue, probably ThO(<a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">OH</a>,<a href="/wiki/Chloride" title="Chloride">Cl</a>)H, is left behind.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_7-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Finely divided thorium metal presents a fire hazard due to its <a href="/wiki/Pyrophoric" title="Pyrophoric" class="mw-redirect">pyrophoricity</a> and must therefore be handled carefully.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_7-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> When heated in air, thorium <a href="/wiki/Swarf" title="Swarf">turnings</a> ignite and burn brilliantly with a white light to produce the dioxide. In bulk, the reaction of pure thorium with air is slow, although corrosion may eventually occur after several months; most thorium samples are however contaminated with varying degrees of the dioxide, which greatly accelerates corrosion.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_7-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Such samples slowly tarnish in air, becoming gray and finally black.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_7-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The most important <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a> of thorium is +4, represented in compounds such as thorium dioxide (ThO<sub>2</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/Thorium_tetrafluoride" title="Thorium tetrafluoride">thorium tetrafluoride</a> (ThF<sub>4</sub>), although some compounds are known with thorium in lower formal oxidation states.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6466_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6466-13"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7077_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7077-14"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Owing to thorium(IV)'s lack of <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> on <a href="/wiki/Electron_configuration" title="Electron configuration">6d and 5f orbitals</a>, the tetravalent thorium compounds are colorless.<sup id="cite_ref-Yu._D._Tretyakov_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yu._D._Tretyakov-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In aqueous solution, thorium occurs exclusively as the tetrapositive <a href="/wiki/Aqua_ion" title="Aqua ion" class="mw-redirect">aqua ion</a> [Th(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>9</sub>]<sup>4+</sup>, which has <a href="/wiki/Tricapped_trigonal_prismatic_molecular_geometry" title="Tricapped trigonal prismatic molecular geometry">tricapped trigonal prismatic molecular geometry</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder117134_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder117134-16"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> at pH&nbsp;&lt;&nbsp;3, the solutions of thorium salts are dominated by this cation.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder117134_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder117134-16"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> The Th–O bond distance is (245&nbsp;±&nbsp;1)&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Picometer" title="Picometer" class="mw-redirect">pm</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Coordination_number" title="Coordination number">coordination number</a> of Th<sup>4+</sup> is (10.8&nbsp;±&nbsp;0.5), the effective charge is 3.82 and the second coordination sphere contains 13.4 water molecules.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder117134_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder117134-16"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> The Th<sup>4+</sup> ion is relatively large and is the largest of the tetrapositive actinide ions, and depending on the <a href="/wiki/Coordination_number" title="Coordination number">coordination number</a> can have a radius between 0.95&nbsp;and&nbsp;1.14&nbsp;Å. As a result, thorium salts have a weak tendency to hydrolyze, weaker than that of many multiply charged ions such as <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">Fe</a><sup>3+</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder117134_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder117134-16"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> The distinctive ability of thorium salts is their high solubility, not only in water, but also in polar organic solvents.<sup id="cite_ref-Yu._D._Tretyakov_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yu._D._Tretyakov-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Thorium has been shown to activate <a href="/wiki/Carbon%E2%80%93hydrogen_bond" title="Carbon–hydrogen bond">carbon–hydrogen bonds</a>, forming unusual compounds. Thorium atoms can also bond to more atoms than any other element: for instance, in the compound <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium_aminodiboranate&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Thorium aminodiboranate (page does not exist)">thorium aminodiboranate</a>, thorium has a <a href="/wiki/Coordination_number" title="Coordination number">coordination number</a> of fifteen.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atomic">Atomic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Atomic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>A thorium atom has 90 electrons, of which four are <a href="/wiki/Valence_electron" title="Valence electron">valence electrons</a>. Four <a href="/wiki/Atomic_orbital" title="Atomic orbital">atomic orbitals</a> are theoretically available for the valence electrons to occupy: 5f, 6d, 7s, and 7p. However, the 7p orbital is greatly destabilized and hence it is not occupied in the ground state of any thorium ion.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder5960_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder5960-19"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite thorium's position in the <a href="/wiki/F-block" title="F-block" class="mw-redirect">f-block</a> of the periodic table, it has an anomalous [Rn]6d<sup>2</sup>7s<sup>2</sup> <a href="/wiki/Electron_configuration" title="Electron configuration">electron configuration</a> in the ground state. However, in metallic thorium, the [Rn]5f<sup>1</sup>6d<sup>1</sup>7s<sup>2</sup> configuration is a low-lying <a href="/wiki/Excited_state" title="Excited state">excited state</a> and hence the 5f orbitals contribute, existing in a rather broad <a href="/wiki/Electronic_band_structure" title="Electronic band structure">energy band</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder5960_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder5960-19"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The ground-state electron configurations of thorium ions are as follows: Th<sup>+</sup>, [Rn]6d<sup>2</sup>7s<sup>1</sup>; Th<sup>2+</sup>, [Rn]5f<sup>1</sup>6d<sup>1</sup>;<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>c<span>]</span></a></sup> Th<sup>3+</sup>, [Rn]5f<sup>1</sup>; Th<sup>4+</sup>, [Rn]. This shows the increasing stabilization of the 5f orbital as ion charge increases; however, this stabilization is insufficient to chemically stabilize Th<sup>3+</sup> with its lone 5f valence electron, and therefore the stable and most common form of thorium in chemicals is Th<sup>4+</sup> with all four valence electrons lost, leaving behind an inert core of inner electrons with the electron configuration of the <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gas</a> <a href="/wiki/Radon" title="Radon">radon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder5960_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder5960-19"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> The first <a href="/wiki/Ionization_potential" title="Ionization potential" class="mw-redirect">ionization potential</a> of thorium was measured to be (6.08&nbsp;±&nbsp;0.12)&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">eV</a> in 1974;<sup id="cite_ref-NIST_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIST-22"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> more recent measurements have refined this to 6.3067&nbsp;eV.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_thorium" title="Isotopes of thorium">Isotopes of thorium</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Although thorium has 6 naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>, none of these isotopes are stable; however, one isotope, <sup>232</sup>Th, is relatively stable, with a half-life of 14.05&nbsp;billion&nbsp;years, considerably longer than the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_earth" title="Age of the earth" class="mw-redirect">age of the earth</a>, and even slightly longer than the generally accepted <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_universe" title="Age of the universe">age of the universe</a> (about 13.8&nbsp;billion&nbsp;years).<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>d<span>]</span></a></sup> This isotope is the longest-lived of all isotopes with more than <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">83 protons</a> and makes up nearly all natural thorium. As such, thorium is most commonly considered to be <a href="/wiki/Mononuclidic_elements" title="Mononuclidic elements" class="mw-redirect">mononuclidic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NUBASE_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-24"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> However, in deep <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">seawaters</a> the isotope <sup>230</sup>Th becomes significant enough that <a href="/wiki/IUPAC" title="IUPAC" class="mw-redirect">IUPAC</a> reclassified thorium as a binuclidic element in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-IUPAC2013_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUPAC2013-28"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> In fact, uranium ores with low thorium concentrations can be purified to produce gram-sized thorium samples of which over a quarter is the <sup>230</sup>Th isotope.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder535_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder535-29"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> Thorium has a characteristic terrestrial isotopic composition, consisting largely of <sup>232</sup>Th and a little <sup>230</sup>Th, and thus an <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass" title="Atomic mass">atomic mass</a> can be given, which is 232.0377(4)&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Unified_atomic_mass_unit" title="Unified atomic mass unit" class="mw-redirect">u</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUPAC2013_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUPAC2013-28"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p><sup>232</sup>Th is the longest-lived isotope in the 4<i>n</i> decay chain which includes isotopes with a <a href="/wiki/Mass_number" title="Mass number">mass number</a> divisible by 4, begins with the alpha decay of <sup>232</sup>Th to <sup>228</sup>Ra,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>e<span>]</span></a></sup> and terminates at stable <a href="/wiki/Lead-208" title="Lead-208" class="mw-redirect"><sup>208</sup>Pb</a>, and its longevity means that the isotopes in its decay chain still exist in nature as <a href="/wiki/Radiogenic_nuclide" title="Radiogenic nuclide">radiogenic nuclides</a> despite their short half-lives.<sup id="cite_ref-NUBASE_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-24"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>f<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>g<span>]</span></a></sup> As such, natural thorium samples can be chemically purified to extract its useful daughter nuclides, such as <a href="/wiki/Lead-212" title="Lead-212" class="mw-redirect">lead-212</a> (<sup>212</sup>Pb), which is used in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_medicine" title="Nuclear medicine">nuclear medicine</a> for <a href="/wiki/Cancer_therapy" title="Cancer therapy" class="mw-redirect">cancer therapy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Thirty <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope" title="Radioisotope" class="mw-redirect">radioisotopes</a> have been characterized, which range in <a href="/wiki/Mass_number" title="Mass number">mass number</a> from 209<sup id="cite_ref-Ikezoe_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ikezoe-35"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> to 238.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder535_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder535-29"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> The most stable of them (after <sup>232</sup>Th) are <sup>230</sup>Th with a half-life of 75,380&nbsp;years, <sup>229</sup>Th with a half-life of 7,340&nbsp;years, <sup>228</sup>Th with a half-life of 1.92&nbsp;years, <sup>234</sup>Th with a half-life of 24.10&nbsp;days, and <sup>227</sup>Th with a half-life of 18.68&nbsp;days: all of these isotopes occur in nature as <a href="/wiki/Trace_radioisotope" title="Trace radioisotope">trace radioisotopes</a> due to their presence in the decay chains of <sup>232</sup>Th, <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235"><sup>235</sup>U</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238"><sup>238</sup>U</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neptunium-237" title="Neptunium-237" class="mw-redirect"><sup>237</sup>Np</a> (produced in minute traces in nuclear reactions in uranium ores). All of the remaining thorium isotopes have half-lives that are less than thirty days and the majority of these have half-lives that are less than ten minutes. The isotope <sup>229</sup>Th has a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">nuclear isomer</a> (or metastable state) with a remarkably low <a href="/w/index.php?title=Excitation_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Excitation energy (page does not exist)">excitation energy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Ruchowska_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ruchowska-36"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> recently measured to be (7.6&nbsp;±&nbsp;0.5)&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">eV</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Beck_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beck-37"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In the early history of the study of radioactivity, the different natural isotopes of thorium were given different names. In this scheme, <sup>227</sup>Th was named radioactinium (RdAc), <sup>228</sup>Th radiothorium (RdTh), <sup>230</sup>Th ionium (Io), <sup>231</sup>Th uranium Y (UY), <sup>232</sup>Th thorium (Th), and <sup>234</sup>Th uranium X1 (UX<sub>1</sub>).<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder535_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder535-29"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> When it was realized that all of these are isotopes of thorium, many of these names fell out of use, and "thorium" came to refer to all isotopes, not just <sup>232</sup>Th.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder535_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder535-29"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> However, the name ionium is still encountered for <sup>230</sup>Th in the context of <a href="/wiki/Ionium-thorium_dating" title="Ionium-thorium dating">ionium-thorium dating</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rafferty2010_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rafferty2010-38"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vertes2010_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vertes2010-39"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Different isotopes of thorium behave identically chemically, but do have slightly differing physical properties: for example, the densities of <sup>228</sup>Th, <sup>229</sup>Th, <sup>230</sup>Th, and <sup>232</sup>Th in g·cm<sup>−3</sup> are respectively expected to be 11.524, 11.575, 11.626, and 11.727.<sup id="cite_ref-critical_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-critical-40"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> The isotope <sup>229</sup>Th is expected to be <a href="/wiki/Fissionable" title="Fissionable" class="mw-redirect">fissionable</a> with a bare critical mass of 2839&nbsp;kg, although with <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> reflectors this value could drop to 994&nbsp;kg.<sup id="cite_ref-critical_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-critical-40"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> While <sup>232</sup>Th, the most common thorium isotope, is not fissionable, it is <a href="/wiki/Fertile_material" title="Fertile material">fertile</a> as it can be converted to fissile <a href="/wiki/Uranium-233" title="Uranium-233"><sup>233</sup>U</a> using <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">neutron capture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-critical_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-critical-40"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-41"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="220" height="318" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3063" data-file-height="4430"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Thor, the namesake of thorium
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In 1815, the Swedish chemist <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jakob_Berzelius" title="Jöns Jakob Berzelius" class="mw-redirect">Jöns Jakob Berzelius</a> analyzed a mineral from a copper mine in <a href="/wiki/Falun" title="Falun">Falun</a>. Assuming that a new element was contained in the mineral, he named the supposed element "thorium" after <a href="/wiki/Thor" title="Thor">Thor</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Norse_god" title="Norse god" class="mw-redirect">Norse god</a> of thunder. However, the mineral later proved to actually be an <a href="/wiki/Yttrium" title="Yttrium">yttrium</a> mineral, primarily composed of <a href="/wiki/Yttrium_phosphate" title="Yttrium phosphate" class="mw-redirect">yttrium orthophosphate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-41"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> As the yttrium in this mineral was initially mistaken as being a new element, the mineral was named <a href="/wiki/Xenotime" title="Xenotime">kenotime</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> words κενός (vain) and τιμή (honor), which became "xenotime" as a misprint.<sup id="cite_ref-Mindat_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mindat-42"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Handbook_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Handbook-43"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lost_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lost-44"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1828, <a href="/wiki/Morten_Thrane_Esmark" title="Morten Thrane Esmark">Morten Thrane Esmark</a> found a black mineral on <a href="/wiki/L%C3%B8v%C3%B8ya,_Telemark" title="Løvøya, Telemark">Løvøya</a> island, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, and gave a sample to his father, <a href="/wiki/Jens_Esmark" title="Jens Esmark">Jens Esmark</a>, a noted <a href="/wiki/Mineralogist" title="Mineralogist" class="mw-redirect">mineralogist</a>. The elder Esmark was not able to identify it and sent a sample to the Swedish chemist <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jakob_Berzelius" title="Jöns Jakob Berzelius" class="mw-redirect">Jöns Jakob Berzelius</a> for examination. Berzelius determined that it contained a new element.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-41"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> He published his findings in 1829.<sup id="cite_ref-Weeks_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks-45"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> Berzelius reused the name of the previous supposed element discovery.<sup id="cite_ref-Weeks_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks-45"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> Thus, he named the source mineral <a href="/wiki/Thorite" title="Thorite">thorite</a>, which has the chemical composition (Th,<a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">U</a>)<a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">SiO<sub>4</sub></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-41"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subsequent_developments">Subsequent developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Subsequent developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>In <a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Mendeleev" title="Dmitri Mendeleev">Dmitri Mendeleev</a>'s 1869 periodic table, thorium and the <a href="/wiki/Rare_earth_element" title="Rare earth element">rare earth elements</a> were placed outside the main body of the table, at the end of each vertical period after the <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth_metal" title="Alkaline earth metal">alkaline earth metals</a>. This reflected the belief at that time that thorium and the rare earth metals were divalent.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>h<span>]</span></a></sup> With the later recognition that the rare earths were mostly trivalent and thorium was tetravalent, Mendeleev moved <a href="/wiki/Cerium" title="Cerium">cerium</a> and thorium to group IV in 1871, which contained the modern <a href="/wiki/Carbon_group" title="Carbon group">carbon group</a>, <a href="/wiki/Group_4_element" title="Group 4 element">titanium group</a>, cerium, and thorium, because their maximum <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a> was +4.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jensen_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jensen-51"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> While cerium was soon removed from the main body of the table and placed in a separate <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanide</a> series, it was not until 1945 that <a href="/wiki/Glenn_T._Seaborg" title="Glenn T. Seaborg">Glenn T. Seaborg</a> realized that thorium was the second member of the actinide series and was filling an f-block row, instead of being the heavier congener of <a href="/wiki/Hafnium" title="Hafnium">hafnium</a> and filling a fourth d-block row.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>i<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Thorium was first observed to be radioactive in 1898, independently, by the Polish-French physicist <a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie" title="Marie Curie">Marie Curie</a> and the German chemist <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Carl_Schmidt" title="Gerhard Carl Schmidt">Gerhard Carl Schmidt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> Between 1900 and 1903, <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Soddy" title="Frederick Soddy">Frederick Soddy</a> showed how thorium decayed at a fixed rate over time into a series of other elements. This observation led to the identification of <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> as one of the outcomes of the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particle</a> experiments that led to their disintegration theory of <a href="/wiki/Radioactivity" title="Radioactivity" class="mw-redirect">radioactivity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Although thorium was discovered in 1828, it had no applications until 1885, when <a href="/wiki/Carl_Auer_von_Welsbach" title="Carl Auer von Welsbach">Carl Auer von Welsbach</a> invented the <a href="/wiki/Gas_mantle" title="Gas mantle">gas mantle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_41-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-41"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> After 1885, many applications were found for thorium and its compounds. In recent decades, however, most of these applications that do not depend on thorium's radioactivity have declined due to safety and environmental concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_41-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-41"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Occurrence_of_thorium" title="Occurrence of thorium">Occurrence of thorium</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.jpg/350px-Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.jpg" width="350" height="263" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.jpg/525px-Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.jpg/700px-Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1125"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> The 
        <a href="/wiki/Radiogenic_heat" title="Radiogenic heat" class="mw-redirect">radiogenic heat</a> from the decay of 
        <sup>232</sup>Th is a major contributor to the 
        <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_internal_heat_budget" title="Earth's internal heat budget">earth's internal heat budget</a>. The other major contributors are 
        <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235"><sup>235</sup>U</a>, 
        <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238"><sup>238</sup>U</a>, and 
        <a href="/wiki/Potassium-40" title="Potassium-40"><sup>40</sup>K</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Thorium-232 is a <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">primordial nuclide</a>, having existed in its current form for <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth" title="Age of the Earth">over 4.5 billion years</a>, predating the <a href="/wiki/Formation_of_the_Earth" title="Formation of the Earth" class="mw-redirect">formation of the Earth</a>; it was forged in the cores of dying stars through the <a href="/wiki/R-process" title="R-process">r-process</a> and scattered across the galaxy by <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovae</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> Its <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a> produces a significant amount of the <a href="/wiki/Earth#Heat" title="Earth">Earth's internal heat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NGJuly11_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NGJuly11-60"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Natural thorium is essentially isotopically pure <sup>232</sup>Th, which is the longest-lived and most stable isotope of thorium, having a half-life comparable to the age of the universe. If the source contains no uranium, the only other thorium isotope present would be <sup>228</sup>Th, which occurs in the <a href="/wiki/Decay_chain" title="Decay chain">decay chain</a> of thorium-232 (the <a href="/wiki/Thorium_series" title="Thorium series" class="mw-redirect">thorium series</a>): the ratio of <sup>228</sup>Th to <sup>232</sup>Th would be under 10<sup>−10</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder535_29-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder535-29"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> However, if uranium is present, tiny traces of several other isotopes will be present: <sup>231</sup>Th and <sup>227</sup>Th from the decay chain of <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">uranium-235</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Actinium_series" title="Actinium series" class="mw-redirect">actinium series</a>), and slightly larger but still tiny traces of <sup>234</sup>Th and <sup>230</sup>Th from the decay chain of <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238">uranium-238</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Uranium_series" title="Uranium series" class="mw-redirect">uranium series</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder535_29-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder535-29"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> <sup>229</sup>Th is also been produced in the decay chain of <a href="/wiki/Neptunium" title="Neptunium"><sup>237</sup>Np</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Neptunium_series" title="Neptunium series" class="mw-redirect">neptunium series</a>): while all primordial <sup>237</sup>Np is <a href="/wiki/Extinct_radionuclide" title="Extinct radionuclide">extinct</a>, it is still produced today as a result of nuclear reactions in uranium ores.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> <sup>229</sup>Th is mostly produced as a <a href="/wiki/Decay_product" title="Decay product">daughter</a> of artificial <a href="/wiki/Uranium-233" title="Uranium-233">uranium-233</a>, itself produced from <a href="/wiki/Neutron_irradiation" title="Neutron irradiation" class="mw-redirect">neutron irradiation</a> of <sup>232</sup>Th, due to its extreme rarity in nature.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder535_29-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder535-29"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>On Earth, thorium is not a rare element as was previously thought, having an abundance comparable to that of <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> and <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a>, twice that of <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a>, and thrice that of <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder556_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder556-62"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> In nature, it occurs in the +4 oxidation state, together with uranium(IV), <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">zirconium</a>(IV), <a href="/wiki/Hafnium" title="Hafnium">hafnium</a>(IV), and cerium(IV), but also with the <a href="/wiki/Scandium" title="Scandium">scandium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yttrium" title="Yttrium">yttrium</a>, and the trivalent lanthanides which have similar <a href="/wiki/Ionic_radius" title="Ionic radius">ionic radii</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder556_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder556-62"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> However, thorium only occurs as a minor constituent of most minerals.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder556_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder556-62"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Thorium minerals occur on all continents.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-11"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-usgssumm_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usgssumm-64"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> Thorium is several times more abundant in Earth's crust than all <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_uranium" title="Isotopes of uranium">isotopes of uranium</a> combined and thorium-232 is several hundred times more abundant than uranium-235.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder556_62-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder556-62"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> Because of thorium's radioactivity, minerals containing significant quantities of thorium are often <a href="/wiki/Metamictization" title="Metamictization">metamict</a>, their crystal structure having been partially or totally destroyed by the alpha radiation produced in the radioactive decay of thorium.<sup id="cite_ref-Woodhead_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woodhead-65"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>j<span>]</span></a></sup> An extreme example is <a href="/wiki/Ekanite" title="Ekanite">ekanite</a> ((Ca,Fe,Pb)<sub>2</sub>(Th,U)Si<sub>8</sub>O<sub>20</sub>), which almost never occurs in nonmetamict form due to thorium being an essential part of its chemical composition.<sup id="cite_ref-ekanite_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ekanite-66"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Monazite" title="Monazite">Monazite</a> is the most important commercial source of thorium because it occurs in large deposits worldwide and contains 2.5% thorium. It is a chemically unreactive <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> mineral that has a high <a href="/wiki/Specific_gravity" title="Specific gravity">specific gravity</a> and is found as yellow or brown monazite sand; its low reactivity makes it difficult to extract thorium from it.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder556_62-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder556-62"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Allanite" title="Allanite">Allanite</a> can have 0.1–2% thorium and <a href="/wiki/Zircon" title="Zircon">zircon</a> up to 0.4% thorium.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder556_62-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder556-62"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Thorium dioxide occurs as the rare mineral <a href="/wiki/Thorianite" title="Thorianite">thorianite</a>, which usually contains up to 12% ThO<sub>2</sub>. However, due to its being <a href="/wiki/Isotypic" title="Isotypic" class="mw-redirect">isotypic</a> with <a href="/wiki/Uranium_dioxide" title="Uranium dioxide">uranium dioxide</a>, the two actinide dioxides can form solid-state solutions and the name of the mineral changes according to the ThO<sub>2</sub> content.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder556_62-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder556-62"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>k<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thorite" title="Thorite">Thorite</a>, or <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium_silicate&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Thorium silicate (page does not exist)">thorium silicate</a> (ThSiO<sub>4</sub>),<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>l<span>]</span></a></sup> also has a high thorium content and is the mineral in which thorium was first discovered.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder556_62-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder556-62"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> In thorium silicate minerals, the Th<sup>4+</sup> and <span class="chemf nowrap">SiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:85%;text-align:left">4−<br> 4</span></span> ions are often replaced with M<sup>3+</sup> (M = <a href="/wiki/Scandium" title="Scandium">Sc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yttrium" title="Yttrium">Y</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">Ln</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">PO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:85%;text-align:left">3−<br> 4</span></span>) ions respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder556_62-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder556-62"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>m<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:MonaziteUSGOV.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/MonaziteUSGOV.jpg/220px-MonaziteUSGOV.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/MonaziteUSGOV.jpg/330px-MonaziteUSGOV.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8e/MonaziteUSGOV.jpg 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="270"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:MonaziteUSGOV.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Monazite – a major thorium mineral
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Thorium is extracted mostly from <a href="/wiki/Monazite" title="Monazite">monazite</a>: thorium diphosphate (Th(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>) is reacted with <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a>, and the produced thorium nitrate treated with <a href="/wiki/Tributyl_phosphate" title="Tributyl phosphate">tributyl phosphate</a>. <a href="/wiki/Rare_earth_element" title="Rare earth element">Rare-earth</a> impurities are separated by increasing the <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> in sulfate solution.<sup id="cite_ref-g215_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-g215-71"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In another extraction method, monazite is decomposed with a 45% aqueous solution of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">sodium hydroxide</a> at 140&nbsp;°C. Mixed metal hydroxides are extracted first, filtered at 80&nbsp;°C, washed with water and dissolved with concentrated <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>. Next, the acidic solution is neutralized with hydroxides to pH = 5.8 that results in precipitation of thorium hydroxide (Th(OH)<sub>4</sub>) contaminated with ~3% of rare-earth hydroxides; the remaining rare-earth hydroxides remain in solution. Thorium hydroxide is dissolved in an inorganic acid and then purified from the <a href="/wiki/Rare_earth_element" title="Rare earth element">rare earth elements</a>. An efficient method is the dissolution of thorium hydroxide in nitric acid, because the resulting solution can be purified by <a href="/wiki/Liquid-liquid_extraction" title="Liquid-liquid extraction" class="mw-redirect">extraction</a> with organic solvents:<sup id="cite_ref-g215_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-g215-71"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       Th(OH)
       <sub>4</sub> + 4 HNO
       <sub>3</sub> → Th(NO
       <sub>3</sub>)
       <sub>4</sub> + 4 H
       <sub>2</sub>O
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <p>Metallic thorium is separated from the anhydrous oxide or chloride by reacting it with calcium in an inert atmosphere:<sup id="cite_ref-g1255_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-g1255-72"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       ThO
       <sub>2</sub> + 2 Ca → 2 CaO + Th
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <p>Sometimes thorium is extracted by <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> of a fluoride in a mixture of sodium and potassium chloride at 700–800&nbsp;°C in a <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a> crucible. Highly pure thorium can be extracted from its iodide with the <a href="/wiki/Crystal_bar_process" title="Crystal bar process">crystal bar process</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxides_and_hydroxides">Oxides and hydroxides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Oxides and hydroxides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/Thorium_dioxide" title="Thorium dioxide">Thorium dioxide</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:CaF2_polyhedra.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/CaF2_polyhedra.png/250px-CaF2_polyhedra.png" width="250" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/CaF2_polyhedra.png/375px-CaF2_polyhedra.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/CaF2_polyhedra.png/500px-CaF2_polyhedra.png 2x" data-file-width="1417" data-file-height="945"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:CaF2_polyhedra.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Thorium dioxide has the 
        <a href="/wiki/Fluorite" title="Fluorite">fluorite</a> structure. Th
        <sup>4+</sup>: 
        <span style="color:silver; background:silver;">__</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;/&nbsp;&nbsp;O
        <sup>2−</sup>: 
        <span style="color:#9c0; background:#9c0;">__</span>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In air, thorium turnings burn to form the simple dioxide, ThO<sub>2</sub>, also called <a href="/wiki/Thoria" title="Thoria" class="mw-redirect">thoria</a> or thorina.<sup id="cite_ref-Yamashita_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yamashita-74"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> Thoria, a refractory material, has the highest <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">melting point</a> (3390&nbsp;°C) of all known oxides.<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley-75"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> It is somewhat <a href="/wiki/Hygroscopic" title="Hygroscopic" class="mw-redirect">hygroscopic</a> and reacts readily with water and many gases.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7077_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7077-14"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> When heated, it emits intense blue light, which becomes white when mixed with its lighter homolog <a href="/wiki/Cerium_dioxide" title="Cerium dioxide" class="mw-redirect">cerium dioxide</a> (CeO<sub>2</sub>, ceria): this is the basis for its previously common application in <a href="/wiki/Gas_mantle" title="Gas mantle">gas mantles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7077_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7077-14"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Reports of thorium <a href="/wiki/Peroxide" title="Peroxide">peroxide</a>, initially supposed to be Th<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> and be formed from reacting thorium salts with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">hydrogen peroxide</a>, were later discovered to contain both peroxide anions and the anions of the reacting thorium salt.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7077_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7077-14"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Thorium monoxide has recently been produced through <a href="/wiki/Laser_ablation" title="Laser ablation">laser ablation</a> of thorium in the presence of oxygen.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> This highly polar molecule has the largest known internal electric field.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium_hydroxide&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Thorium hydroxide (page does not exist)">Thorium hydroxide</a>, Th(OH)<sub>4</sub>, can be prepared by adding a <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxide</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ammonium" title="Ammonium">ammonium</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali metal</a> to a thorium <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salt</a> solution, where it appears as a gelatinous precipitate that will dissolve in dilute acids, among other substances.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7077_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7077-14"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> It can also be prepared by <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> of thorium <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7077_14-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7077-14"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> It is stable from 260–450&nbsp;°C; at 470&nbsp;°C and above it continuously decomposes to become thoria.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7077_14-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7077-14"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> It easily absorbs atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> to form the hydrated <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a> ThOCO<sub>3</sub>·<i>x</i>H<sub>2</sub>O and, under high-pressure conditions in a carbon dioxide atmosphere, Th(CO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·0.5H<sub>2</sub>O or Th(OH)<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7077_14-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7077-14"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder101115_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder101115-78"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Halides">Halides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Halides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Kristallstruktur_Uran(IV)-fluorid.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Kristallstruktur_Uran%28IV%29-fluorid.png/220px-Kristallstruktur_Uran%28IV%29-fluorid.png" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Kristallstruktur_Uran%28IV%29-fluorid.png/330px-Kristallstruktur_Uran%28IV%29-fluorid.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Kristallstruktur_Uran%28IV%29-fluorid.png/440px-Kristallstruktur_Uran%28IV%29-fluorid.png 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="900"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Kristallstruktur_Uran(IV)-fluorid.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Crystal structure of thorium tetrafluoride. Th
        <sup>4+</sup>: 
        <span style="color:silver; background:silver;">__</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;/&nbsp;&nbsp;F
        <sup>−</sup>: 
        <span style="color:#9c0; background:#9c0;">__</span>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>All four thorium tetrahalides are known, as are some low-valent bromides and iodides.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Additionally, many related polyhalide ions are also known.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thorium_tetrafluoride" title="Thorium tetrafluoride">Thorium tetrafluoride</a> (ThF<sub>4</sub>) is most easily produced by reacting various thorium salts, thoria, or thorium hydroxide with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fluoride" title="Hydrogen fluoride">hydrogen fluoride</a>: methods that involve steps in the aqueous phase are more difficult because they result in hydroxide and oxide fluorides that have to be reduced with hydrogen fluoride or <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a> gas.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> It has a <a href="/wiki/Monoclinic_crystal_system" title="Monoclinic crystal system">monoclinic</a> crystal structure and is <a href="/wiki/Isotypic" title="Isotypic" class="mw-redirect">isotypic</a> with <a href="/wiki/Zirconium_tetrafluoride" title="Zirconium tetrafluoride">zirconium tetrafluoride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hafnium_tetrafluoride" title="Hafnium tetrafluoride">hafnium tetrafluoride</a>, where the Th<sup>4+</sup> ions are coordinated with F<sup>−</sup> ions in somewhat distorted <a href="/wiki/Square_antiprism" title="Square antiprism">square antiprisms</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_15-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> It is a white, <a href="/wiki/Hygroscopic" title="Hygroscopic" class="mw-redirect">hygroscopic</a> powder: at temperatures above 500&nbsp;°C, it reacts with atmospheric moisture to produce the oxyfluoride ThOF<sub>2</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Thorium_tetrachloride" title="Thorium tetrachloride" class="mw-redirect">Thorium tetrachloride</a> (ThCl<sub>4</sub>) can be produced in many ways. The usual method is <a href="/wiki/Crystallization" title="Crystallization">crystallization</a> from an aqueous solution and then heating the product above 100&nbsp;°C to dehydrate it.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_15-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Further purification can be achieved by <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(phase_transition)" title="Sublimation (phase transition)">subliming</a> it. Its melting and <a href="/wiki/Boiling_point" title="Boiling point">boiling points</a> are respectively 770&nbsp;°C and 921&nbsp;°C.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_15-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> It undergoes a <a href="/wiki/Phase_transition" title="Phase transition">phase transition</a> at 405&nbsp;°C, with a low-temperature α phase and high-temperature β phase. Nevertheless, the β phase usually persists below the transition temperature. Both phases crystallize in the <a href="/wiki/Tetragonal_crystal_system" title="Tetragonal crystal system">tetragonal crystal system</a> and the structural differences are small.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_15-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Below −203&nbsp;°C, a low-temperature form exists with a complex structure.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_15-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Thorium tetrabromide (ThBr<sub>4</sub>) can be produced either by reacting thorium(IV) hydroxide with <a href="/wiki/Hydrobromic_acid" title="Hydrobromic acid">hydrobromic acid</a> (which has the disadvantage of often resulting in products contaminated with oxybromides) or by directly reacting <a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">bromine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bromide" title="Hydrogen bromide">hydrogen bromide</a> with thorium metal or compounds.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_15-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> The product can then be purified by sublimation at 600&nbsp;°C in a vacuum.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_15-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> The melting and boiling points are 679&nbsp;°C and 857&nbsp;°C.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_15-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Like the tetrachloride, both an α and a β form exist and both are isotypic to the tetrachloride forms, though the phase transition here occurs at 426&nbsp;°C. There is also a low-temperature form.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_15-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Incomplete reports of the lower bromides ThBr<sub>3</sub>, ThBr<sub>2</sub>, and ThBr are known (the last only known as a gas-phase molecular species): ThBr<sub>3</sub> and ThBr<sub>2</sub> are known to be very reactive and at high temperatures <a href="/wiki/Disproportionation_reaction" title="Disproportionation reaction" class="mw-redirect">disproportionate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_15-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Thorium_tetraiodide" title="Thorium tetraiodide" class="mw-redirect">Thorium tetraiodide</a> (ThI<sub>4</sub>) is prepared by direct reaction of the elements in a sealed <a href="/wiki/Silica" title="Silica" class="mw-redirect">silica</a> <a href="/wiki/Ampoule" title="Ampoule">ampoule</a>. Water and oxygen must not be present, or else ThOI<sub>2</sub> and ThO<sub>2</sub> can contaminate the product.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_15-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> It has a different crystal structure from the other tetrahalides, being <a href="/wiki/Monoclinic_crystal_system" title="Monoclinic crystal system">monoclinic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_15-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> The lower iodides ThI<sub>3</sub> and ThI<sub>2</sub> can be prepared by reducing the tetraiodide with thorium metal. (ThI is also predicted to form as an intermediate in the dissociation of ThI<sub>4</sub> to thorium metal.)<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_15-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> These do not contain Th(III) and Th(II), but instead contain Th<sup>4+</sup> and could be more clearly formulated as <span class="nowrap">Th<sup>4+</sup>(I<sup>−</sup>)<sub>3</sub>(e<sup>−</sup>)</span> and <span class="nowrap">Th<sup>4+</sup>(I<sup>−</sup>)<sub>2</sub>(e<sup>−</sup>)<sub>2</sub></span> respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_15-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Depending on the amount of time allowed for the reaction between ThI<sub>4</sub> and thorium, two modifications of ThI<sub>3</sub> can be produced: shorter times give thin lustrous rods of α-ThI<sub>3</sub>, while longer times give small β-ThI<sub>3</sub> crystals with green to brass-colored luster.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_15-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> ThI<sub>2</sub> also has two modifications, which can be produced by varying the reaction temperature: at 600&nbsp;°C, α-ThI<sub>2</sub> is formed, while a reaction temperature of 700–850&nbsp;°C produces β-ThI<sub>2</sub>, which has a golden luster.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_15-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Many polynary halides with the <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali metals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barium" title="Barium">barium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thallium" title="Thallium">thallium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ammonium" title="Ammonium">ammonium</a> are known for thorium fluorides, chlorides, and bromides.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_15-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> For example, when treated with <a href="/wiki/Potassium_fluoride" title="Potassium fluoride">potassium fluoride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid" title="Hydrofluoric acid">hydrofluoric acid</a>, Th<sup>4+</sup> forms the complex anion <span class="chemf nowrap">ThF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:85%;text-align:left">2−<br> 6</span></span>, which precipitates as an insoluble salt, K<sub>2</sub>ThF<sub>6</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-ekhyde_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ekhyde-12"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chalcogenides_and_pnictides">Chalcogenides and pnictides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Chalcogenides and pnictides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>The heavier <a href="/wiki/Chalcogen" title="Chalcogen">chalcogens</a> <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">selenium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tellurium" title="Tellurium">tellurium</a> are known to form thorium chalcogenides, many of which have more complex structure than the oxides. Apart from several binary compounds, the oxychalcogenides ThOS (yellow), ThOSe, and ThOTe are also known.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder9597_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder9597-80"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> The five binary thorium sulfides – ThS (lustrous metallic), Th<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (brown metallic), Th<sub>7</sub>S<sub>12</sub> (black), ThS<sub>2</sub> (purple-brown), and Th<sub>2</sub>S<sub>5</sub> (orange-brown) – may be produced by reacting <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> with thorium, its halides, or thoria (the last if carbon is present): they all hydrolyze in acidic solutions.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder9597_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder9597-80"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> The six selenides are analogous to the sulfides, with the addition of ThSe<sub>3</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder9597_80-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder9597-80"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> The five tellurides are also similar to the sulfides and selenides (although Th<sub>2</sub>Te<sub>5</sub> is unknown), but have slightly different crystal structures: for example, ThS has the <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a> structure, but ThTe has the <a href="/wiki/Caesium_chloride" title="Caesium chloride">caesium chloride</a> structure.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder9597_80-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder9597-80"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>All five chemically characterized <a href="/wiki/Pnictogen" title="Pnictogen">pnictogens</a> (<a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a>) also form compounds with thorium.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder97101_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder97101-81"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> Three thorium <a href="/wiki/Nitride" title="Nitride">nitrides</a> are known: ThN, Th<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>, and Th<sub>2</sub>N<sub>3</sub>. The brass-colored Th<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> is most easily produced by heating thorium metal in a nitrogen atmosphere. Th<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> and Th<sub>2</sub>N<sub>3</sub> decompose to the golden-yellow ThN, and indeed ThN can often be seen covering the surface of Th<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> samples because Th<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> is hygroscopic and <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a> in the air can decompose it: thin films of ThN are metallic in character and, like all other actinide mononitrides, has the sodium chloride structure. ThN is also a low-temperature superconductor. All three nitrides can react with thorium halides to form halide nitrides ThNX (X = F, Cl, Br, I).<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder97101_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder97101-81"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> The heavier pnictogens also form analogous monopnictides, except ThBi which has not yet been structurally characterized. The other well-characterized thorium pnictides are Th<sub>3</sub>P<sub>4</sub>, Th<sub>2</sub>P<sub>11</sub>, ThP<sub>7</sub>, Th<sub>3</sub>As<sub>4</sub>, ThAs<sub>2</sub>, Th<sub>3</sub>Sb<sub>4</sub>, ThSb<sub>2</sub>, and ThBi<sub>2</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder97101_81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder97101-81"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_inorganic">Other inorganic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Other inorganic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
