     <p>Thorium reacts with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> to form the thorium hydrides ThH<sub>2</sub> and Th<sub>4</sub>H<sub>15</sub>, the latter of which is superconducting below the transition temperature of 7.5–8&nbsp;K; at standard temperature and pressure, it conducts electricity like a metal.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6466_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6466-13"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> Finely divided thorium metal reacts very readily with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> at standard conditions, but large pieces may need to be heated to 300–400&nbsp;°C for a reaction to take place.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6466_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6466-13"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> Around 850&nbsp;°C, the reaction forming first ThH<sub>2</sub> and then Th<sub>4</sub>H<sub>15</sub> occurs without breaking up the structure of the thorium metal.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6466_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6466-13"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> Thorium hydrides react readily with <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> or steam to form thoria, and at 250–350&nbsp;°C quickly react with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_halide" title="Hydrogen halide">hydrogen halides</a>, sulfides, phosphides, and nitrides to form the corresponding thorium binary compounds.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6466_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6466-13"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Three binary thorium <a href="/wiki/Boride" title="Boride">borides</a> are known: ThB<sub>6</sub>, ThB<sub>4</sub>, and ThB<sub>12</sub>. The last is isotypic with <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">U</a>B<sub>12</sub>. While reports of ThB<sub>66</sub> and ThB<sub>76</sub> exist, they may simply be thorium-stabilized boron allotropes.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6670_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6670-82"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> The three known binary thorium <a href="/wiki/Carbide" title="Carbide">carbides</a> are ThC<sub>2</sub>, Th<sub>2</sub>C<sub>3</sub>, and ThC: all are produced by reacting thorium or thoria with <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>. ThC and ThC<sub>2</sub> are refractory solids and have melting points over 2600&nbsp;°C.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6670_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6670-82"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Many other inorganic thorium compounds with polyatomic anions are known, such as the <a href="/wiki/Perchlorate" title="Perchlorate">perchlorates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate">sulfates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfite" title="Sulfite">sulfites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vanadate" title="Vanadate">vanadates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molybdate" title="Molybdate">molybdates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chromate" title="Chromate" class="mw-redirect">chromates</a>, and other oxometallates,<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>n<span>]</span></a></sup> many of which are known in hydrated forms.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder101115_78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder101115-78"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> These are important in thorium purification and the disposal of nuclear waste, but most have not yet been fully characterized, especially on their structural properties.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder101115_78-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder101115-78"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> For example, thorium perchlorate is very water-soluble and crystallizes from acidic solutions as the tetrahydrate Th(ClO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O, while thorium nitrate forms tetra- and pentahydrates, is soluble in water and alcohols, and is an important intermediate in the purification of thorium and its compounds.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder101115_78-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder101115-78"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organometallic_and_other_carbon-containing_compounds">Organometallic and other carbon-containing compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Organometallic and other carbon-containing compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Uranocene-3D-balls.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Uranocene-3D-balls.png/120px-Uranocene-3D-balls.png" width="120" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Uranocene-3D-balls.png/180px-Uranocene-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Uranocene-3D-balls.png/240px-Uranocene-3D-balls.png 2x" data-file-width="891" data-file-height="1100"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Uranocene-3D-balls.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Structure of thorocene
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Like many of the early and middle actinides (thorium through <a href="/wiki/Americium" title="Americium">americium</a>, and also expected for <a href="/wiki/Curium" title="Curium">curium</a>), thorium forms the yellow <a href="/wiki/Cyclooctatetraenide_anion" title="Cyclooctatetraenide anion" class="mw-redirect">cyclooctatetraenide</a> complex Th(C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>8</sub>)<sub>2</sub>, thorocene. It is isotypic with the more well-known analogous uranium compound, <a href="/wiki/Uranocene" title="Uranocene">uranocene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder116117_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder116117-84"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> It can be prepared by reacting <a href="/wiki/Potassium_cyclooctatetraenide" title="Potassium cyclooctatetraenide" class="mw-redirect">K<sub>2</sub>C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>8</sub></a> with thorium tetrachloride in <a href="/wiki/Tetrahydrofuran" title="Tetrahydrofuran">tetrahydrofuran</a> (THF) at the temperature of <a href="/wiki/Dry_ice" title="Dry ice">dry ice</a>, or by reacting thorium tetrafluoride with MgC<sub>8</sub>H<sub>8</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder116117_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder116117-84"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> It is an unstable compound in air and outright decomposes in water or at 190&nbsp;°C.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder116117_84-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder116117-84"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> Many other organothorium compounds are known, many involving the <a href="/wiki/Cyclopentadienyl" title="Cyclopentadienyl">cyclopentadienyl</a> anion.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder116117_84-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder116117-84"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> Some coordination complexes with <a href="/wiki/Carboxylate" title="Carboxylate">carboxylates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acetylacetonate" title="Acetylacetonate" class="mw-redirect">acetylacetonates</a> are also known, although these are not organothorium compounds.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder101115_78-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder101115-78"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear">Nuclear</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Nuclear">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Due to thorium's radioactivity, the most important possible use of thorium is in the <a href="/wiki/Thorium_fuel_cycle" title="Thorium fuel cycle">thorium fuel cycle</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel" title="Nuclear fuel">nuclear fuel</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">radiometric dating</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_41-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-41"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_energy">Nuclear energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Nuclear energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Thorium_fuel_cycle" title="Thorium fuel cycle">Thorium fuel cycle</a>
     </div> 
     <p>In <a href="/wiki/Thermal_breeder_reactor" title="Thermal breeder reactor" class="mw-redirect">thermal breeder reactors</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Fertile_material" title="Fertile material">fertile</a> isotope <sup>232</sup>Th, the most common thorium isotope, is bombarded by <a href="/wiki/Slow_neutron" title="Slow neutron" class="mw-redirect">slow neutrons</a>, undergoing <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">neutron capture</a> to become <sup>233</sup>Th, which undergoes two consecutive beta decays to become first <sup>233</sup>Pa and then the fissile <sup>233</sup>U:<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_41-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-41"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="{}_{\ 90}^{232}\mathrm{Th} + \mathrm{n} \rightarrow {}_{\ 90}^{233} \mathrm{Th} + \gamma\ \xrightarrow{\beta^-}\ {}_{\ 91}^{233}\mathrm{Pa}\ \xrightarrow{\beta^-}\ {}_{\ 92}^{233}\mathrm{U}" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/7/4/7/747d78630011bc07c0bdcb100aaf8165.png">
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <p><sup>233</sup>U is fissile and hence can be used as a nuclear fuel in much the same way as the more-commonly used <sup>235</sup>U or <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-239" title="Plutonium-239"><sup>239</sup>Pu</a>. When <sup>233</sup>U undergoes nuclear fission, the neutrons emitted can strike further <sup>232</sup>Th nuclei, restarting the cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_41-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-41"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> This closely parallels the uranium fuel cycle in <a href="/wiki/Fast_breeder_reactor" title="Fast breeder reactor" class="mw-redirect">fast breeder reactors</a> where <sup>238</sup>U undergoes neutron capture to become <sup>239</sup>U, beta decaying to first <sup>239</sup>Np and then fissile <sup>239</sup>Pu.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> The main advantage of the thorium fuel cycle is that thorium is more abundant than uranium and hence can satisfy world energy demands for longer.</p> 
     <p>An added advantage <sup>233</sup>U and <sup>239</sup>Pu enjoy over all other fissile nuclei (except the naturally occurring <sup>235</sup>U) is that they can be <a href="/wiki/Breeder_reactor" title="Breeder reactor">bred</a> from the naturally-occurring quantity isotopes <sup>232</sup>Th and <sup>238</sup>U.<sup id="cite_ref-Ronen_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ronen-86"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-anucene_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anucene-87"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>o<span>]</span></a></sup> Additionally, <sup>233</sup>U is easily detected, can be mixed with <sup>238</sup>U<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_41-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-41"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> to prevent direct use in nuclear weapons<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_41-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-41"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> and limit <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">nuclear proliferation</a>, and has a higher neutron yield than <sup>239</sup>Pu. Thorium fuels also result in a safer and better-performing <a href="/wiki/Reactor_core" title="Reactor core" class="mw-redirect">reactor core</a><sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_41-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-41"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> because thoria has a higher <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">melting point</a>, higher <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a>, and lower <a href="/wiki/Coefficient_of_thermal_expansion" title="Coefficient of thermal expansion" class="mw-redirect">coefficient of thermal expansion</a> than the now-common fuel <a href="/wiki/Uranium_dioxide" title="Uranium dioxide">uranium dioxide</a> (UO<sub>2</sub>): thoria is also more <a href="/wiki/Chemical_stability" title="Chemical stability">chemically stable</a> as, unlike uranium dioxide, it does not further oxidize.<sup id="cite_ref-Thorium_Fuel_Cycle_.E2.80.93_Potential_Benefits_and_Challenges_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thorium_Fuel_Cycle_.E2.80.93_Potential_Benefits_and_Challenges-89"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <table style="text-align:center; border 1px solid; margin: 1em; float:right;"> 
      <caption>

     </table> 
     <p>A single neutron capture by <sup>238</sup>U would produce <a href="/wiki/Transuranic_element" title="Transuranic element" class="mw-redirect">transuranic</a> waste, along with it fissile <sup>239</sup>Pu, whereas six captures are generally necessary to do so from <sup>232</sup>Th. 98–99% of thorium-cycle fuel nuclei would fission at either <sup>233</sup>U or <sup>235</sup>U, so fewer long-lived transuranics are produced. Because of this, thorium is a potentially attractive alternative to uranium in <a href="/wiki/MOX_fuel" title="MOX fuel">mixed oxide (MOX) fuels</a> to minimize the generation of transuranics and maximize the destruction of plutonium.<sup id="cite_ref-wnn-20130621_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wnn-20130621-90"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> A disadvantage of the thorium fuel cycle is the need to neutron irradiate and process natural <sup>232</sup>Th before these advantages become real, and this requires more advanced technology than the presently used fuels based on uranium and plutonium; nevertheless, advances are being made in this technology.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_41-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-41"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Another common criticism centers around the low commercial viability of the thorium fuel cycle:<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SovacoolValentine2012_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SovacoolValentine2012-92"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> some entities like the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Energy_Agency" title="Nuclear Energy Agency">Nuclear Energy Agency</a> go further and predict that the thorium cycle will never be commercially viable while uranium is available in abundance—a situation which <a href="/wiki/Trevor_Findlay" title="Trevor Findlay">Trevor Findlay</a> predicts will persist "in the coming decades".<sup id="cite_ref-FindlayFindlay2011_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FindlayFindlay2011-94"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1997, the U.S. Energy Department underwrote research into thorium fuel, and research also was begun in 1996 by the <a href="/wiki/International_Atomic_Energy_Agency" title="International Atomic Energy Agency">International Atomic Energy Agency</a> (IAEA), to study the use of thorium reactors. Nuclear scientist <a href="/wiki/Alvin_Radkowsky" title="Alvin Radkowsky">Alvin Radkowsky</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tel_Aviv_University" title="Tel Aviv University">Tel Aviv University</a> in Israel founded a consortium to develop thorium reactors, which included other companies: <a href="/wiki/Raytheon" title="Raytheon">Raytheon</a> Nuclear Inc., <a href="/wiki/Brookhaven_National_Laboratory" title="Brookhaven National Laboratory">Brookhaven National Laboratory</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kurchatov_Institute" title="Kurchatov Institute">Kurchatov Institute</a> in Moscow.<sup id="cite_ref-Radkowsky_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Radkowsky-95"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> Radkowsky was chief scientist in the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_submarine" title="Nuclear submarine">nuclear submarine</a> program directed by <a href="/wiki/Admiral_Hyman_Rickover" title="Admiral Hyman Rickover" class="mw-redirect">Admiral Hyman Rickover</a> and later headed the design team that built the USA's first civilian <a href="/wiki/Shippingport_Atomic_Power_Station" title="Shippingport Atomic Power Station">nuclear power plant at Shippingport, Pennsylvania</a>, which was a scaled-up version of the first naval reactor.<sup id="cite_ref-Radkowsky_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Radkowsky-95"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> The third Shippingport core, initiated in 1977, bred thorium.<sup id="cite_ref-asme-landmark_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-asme-landmark-96"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> Even earlier <a href="/wiki/Thorium_fuel_cycle#List_of_thorium-fueled_reactors" title="Thorium fuel cycle">examples</a> of reactors using fuel with thorium exist, including the first core at the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Point_Energy_Center" title="Indian Point Energy Center">Indian Point Energy Center</a> in 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> However, in most countries uranium was relatively abundant and hence research into thorium fuel waned for a while. A notable exception was <a href="/wiki/India%27s_three-stage_nuclear_power_programme" title="India's three-stage nuclear power programme">India's three-stage nuclear power programme</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> In the twenty-first century thorium's potential for improving proliferation resistance and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_waste" title="Nuclear waste" class="mw-redirect">waste</a> characteristics led to renewed interest in the thorium fuel cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> Currently, some countries such as <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> are developing technology for thorium nuclear reactors.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiometric_dating">Radiometric dating</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Radiometric dating">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <p>Two radiometric dating methods involve thorium isotopes: <a href="/wiki/Uranium-thorium_dating" title="Uranium-thorium dating">uranium-thorium dating</a>, involving the decay of <a href="/wiki/Uranium-234" title="Uranium-234"><sup>234</sup>U</a> to <sup>230</sup>Th (ionium), and <a href="/wiki/Ionium-thorium_dating" title="Ionium-thorium dating">ionium-thorium dating</a>, which measures the ratio of <sup>232</sup>Th to <sup>230</sup>Th. These rely on the fact that <sup>232</sup>Th is a primordial radioisotope, but <sup>230</sup>Th only occurs as an intermediate decay product in the decay chain of <sup>238</sup>U.<sup id="cite_ref-uth_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uth-104"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> Uranium-thorium dating is a relatively short-range process because of the short half-lives of <sup>234</sup>U and <sup>230</sup>Th relative to the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth" title="Age of the Earth">age of the Earth</a>: it is also accompanied by a sister process involving the alpha decay of <sup>235</sup>U into <sup>231</sup>Th, which very quickly becomes the longer-lived <sup>231</sup>Pa, and this process is often used to check the results of uranium-thorium dating. Uranium-thorium dating is commonly used to determine the age of <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a> materials such as <a href="/wiki/Speleothem" title="Speleothem">speleothem</a> or <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">coral</a>, because while uranium is rather soluble in water, thorium and protactinium are not, and so they are selectively precipitated into ocean-floor <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediments</a>, from which their ratios are measured. The scheme has a range of several hundred thousand years.<sup id="cite_ref-uth_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uth-104"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> Ionium-thorium dating is a related process, which exploits the insolubility of thorium (both <sup>232</sup>Th and <sup>230</sup>Th) and thus its presence in ocean sediments to date these sediments by measuring the ratio of <sup>232</sup>Th to <sup>230</sup>Th.<sup id="cite_ref-rafferty2010_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rafferty2010-38"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vertes2010_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vertes2010-39"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> Both of these dating methods assume that the proportion of thorium-230 to thorium-232 is a constant during the time period when the sediment layer was formed, that the sediment did not already contain thorium before contributions from the decay of uranium, and that the thorium cannot shift within the sediment layer.<sup id="cite_ref-rafferty2010_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rafferty2010-38"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vertes2010_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vertes2010-39"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-nuclear">Non-nuclear</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Non-nuclear">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Old_thorium_dioxide_gas_mantle_-_oblong_shape.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Old_thorium_dioxide_gas_mantle_-_oblong_shape.JPG/220px-Old_thorium_dioxide_gas_mantle_-_oblong_shape.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Old_thorium_dioxide_gas_mantle_-_oblong_shape.JPG/330px-Old_thorium_dioxide_gas_mantle_-_oblong_shape.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Old_thorium_dioxide_gas_mantle_-_oblong_shape.JPG/440px-Old_thorium_dioxide_gas_mantle_-_oblong_shape.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="1200"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Old_thorium_dioxide_gas_mantle_-_oblong_shape.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Thorium oxide gas mantle
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Many non-nuclear applications of thorium are becoming obsolete due to environmental concerns largely stemming from the radioactivity of thorium and its decay products.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_41-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-41"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> A notable exception is the use of thoria in <a href="/wiki/Gas_tungsten_arc_welding" title="Gas tungsten arc welding">gas tungsten arc welding</a> (GTAW) to increase the high-temperature strength of tungsten electrodes and improve arc stability.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWickleder200652_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWickleder200652-106"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> The electrodes labeled EWTH-1 contain 1% thoria, while those labeled EWTH-2 contain 2%.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> In electronic equipment, thorium coating of <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> wire improves the <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a> <a href="/wiki/Thermionic_emission" title="Thermionic emission">emission</a> of heated <a href="/wiki/Cathode" title="Cathode">cathodes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-11"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The melting point of thoria is 3300&nbsp;°C – the highest of all known oxides. Only a few elements (including <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>) and a few compounds (including <a href="/wiki/Tantalum_carbide" title="Tantalum carbide">tantalum carbide</a>) have higher melting points.<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley-75"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> This means that when heated to high temperatures, it does not melt, but merely <a href="/wiki/Incandescence" title="Incandescence">glows</a> with an intense blue light; addition of <a href="/wiki/Cerium_dioxide" title="Cerium dioxide" class="mw-redirect">cerium dioxide</a> gives a bright white light.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder7077_14-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7077-14"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> This property of thoria means that thoria and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium_nitrate&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Thorium nitrate (page does not exist)">thorium nitrate</a> are used in <a href="/wiki/Gas_mantle" title="Gas mantle">mantles</a> of portable gas lights, including natural gas lamps, oil lamps and camping lights.<sup id="cite_ref-appl_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-appl-108"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> A study in 1981 estimated that the dose from using a thorium mantle every weekend for a year would be 0.3–0.6&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Millirem" title="Millirem" class="mw-redirect">millirems</a> (mrem), tiny in comparison to the normal annual dose of a few hundred millirems: a person actually ingesting a mantle would receive a dose of 200&nbsp;mrem (2&nbsp;mSv).<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-straightdope_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-straightdope-110"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> However, the radioactivity is a major concern for people involved with the manufacture of mantles, and an issue with contamination of soil around some former factory sites.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> Due to these concerns, some manufacturers have switched to other materials, such as <a href="/wiki/Yttrium" title="Yttrium">yttrium</a>, although these are usually either more expensive or less efficient. Other manufacturers continue to make thorium mantles, but moved their factories to <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing countries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-straightdope_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-straightdope-110"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Yellowing_of_thorium_lenses.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Yellowing_of_thorium_lenses.jpg/220px-Yellowing_of_thorium_lenses.jpg" width="220" height="79" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Yellowing_of_thorium_lenses.jpg/330px-Yellowing_of_thorium_lenses.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Yellowing_of_thorium_lenses.jpg/440px-Yellowing_of_thorium_lenses.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4734" data-file-height="1702"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Yellowing_of_thorium_lenses.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Yellowed thoria lens (left), a similar lens partially de-yellowed with ultraviolet radiation (center), and lens without yellowing (right)
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Thoria is a material for <a href="/wiki/Refraction_(metallurgy)" title="Refraction (metallurgy)">heat-resistant</a> <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">ceramics</a>, as used in high-temperature laboratory <a href="/wiki/Crucible" title="Crucible">crucibles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_41-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-41"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> When added to <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a>, it helps increase <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a> and decrease <a href="/wiki/Dispersion_(optics)" title="Dispersion (optics)">dispersion</a>. Such glass finds application in high-quality <a href="/wiki/Lens_(optics)" title="Lens (optics)">lenses</a> for cameras and scientific instruments.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-11"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> The radiation from these lenses can darken them and turn them yellow over a period of years and degrade film, but the health risks are minimal.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> Yellowed lenses may be restored to their original colorless state with lengthy exposure to intense <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> radiation.</p> 
     <p>Thoria was used to control the grain size of <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> metal used for spirals of electric lamps. Thoriated tungsten elements are found in the filaments of <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_tube" title="Vacuum tube">vacuum tubes</a>, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Magnetron" title="Magnetron" class="mw-redirect">magnetron</a> found in <a href="/wiki/Microwave_oven" title="Microwave oven">microwave oven</a>. Thorium is added because it lowers the effective <a href="/wiki/Work_function" title="Work function">work function</a> with the result that the thoriated tungsten <a href="/wiki/Hot_cathode" title="Hot cathode">thermocathode</a> emits electrons at considerably lower temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_41-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-41"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Thoria has been used as a <a href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst" class="mw-redirect">catalyst</a> in the conversion of <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> to <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_41-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-41"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> <a href="/wiki/Cracking_(chemistry)" title="Cracking (chemistry)">cracking</a> and in producing <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_41-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-41"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> It is the active ingredient of <a href="/wiki/Thorotrast" title="Thorotrast">Thorotrast</a>, which was used as <a href="/wiki/Radiocontrast_agent" title="Radiocontrast agent">radiocontrast agent</a> for <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> diagnostics because of thorium's high opacity to X-rays. This use has been abandoned due to its <a href="/wiki/Carcinogenic" title="Carcinogenic" class="mw-redirect">carcinogenic</a> nature.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-11"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Thorium tetrafluoride is used as an antireflection material in multilayered optical coatings. It has excellent optical transparency in the range of 0.35–12&nbsp;µm, and its radiation is primarily due to <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particles</a>, which can be easily stopped by a thin cover layer of another material.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> Thorium tetrafluoride was also used in manufacturing <a href="/wiki/Carbon_arc_lamp" title="Carbon arc lamp" class="mw-redirect">carbon arc lamps</a>, which provided high-intensity illumination for movie projectors and search lights.<sup id="cite_ref-appl_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-appl-108"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precautions">Precautions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Precautions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png/220px-PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png/330px-PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png/440px-PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png 2x" data-file-width="1831" data-file-height="1237"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Experiment on the effect of radiation (from an unburned thorium gas mantle) on the germination and growth of 
        <a href="/wiki/Timothy-grass" title="Timothy-grass">timothy-grass</a> seed; from 
        <i><a href="/wiki/Popular_Science" title="Popular Science">Popular Science</a></i>, 1909.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>As thorium occurs naturally, it exists in very small quantities almost everywhere on Earth: the average human contains about 100&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Microgram" title="Microgram">micrograms</a> of thorium and typically consumes three micrograms per day of thorium.<sup id="cite_ref-Nature.27s_Building_Blocks_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nature.27s_Building_Blocks-114"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> This exposure is raised for people who live near <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a>, or tin processing factories, thorium deposits, radioactive waste disposal sites, and for those who work in uranium, thorium, tin, or phosphate mining or gas mantle production industries.<sup id="cite_ref-ATSDR_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATSDR-115"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> When thorium is ingested, 99.98% does not remain in the body. Out of the thorium that does remain in the body, three quarters of it accumulates in the <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">skeleton</a>. While absorption through the skin is possible, it is not a likely means of thorium exposure.<sup id="cite_ref-epa_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epa-116"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> Powdered thorium metal is <a href="/wiki/Pyrophoric" title="Pyrophoric" class="mw-redirect">pyrophoric</a> and often ignites spontaneously in air.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_7-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Natural thorium decays very slowly compared to many other radioactive materials, and the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_radiation" title="Alpha radiation" class="mw-redirect">alpha radiation</a> emitted cannot penetrate human skin meaning owning and handling small amounts of thorium, such as a <a href="/wiki/Gas_mantle" title="Gas mantle">gas mantle</a>, is considered safe.<sup id="cite_ref-epa_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epa-116"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> Because <sup>232</sup>Th is only mildly radioactive, but decays to more dangerous radionuclides such as <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radon" title="Radon">radon</a>, emitting alpha and <a href="/wiki/Gamma_radiation" title="Gamma radiation" class="mw-redirect">gamma radiation</a>, a proper assessment of the radiological toxicity of <sup>232</sup>Th must include the contribution of its daughters such as <sup>228</sup>Ra.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> Exposure to an aerosol of thorium, however, can lead to increased risk of <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancers</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lung</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pancreas" title="Pancreas">pancreas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a>, as lungs and other internal organs can be penetrated by alpha radiation.<sup id="cite_ref-epa_116-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epa-116"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> Exposure to thorium internally leads to increased risk of <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a> diseases. Thorium is radioactive and produces a radioactive gas, <a href="/wiki/Radon" title="Radon">radon</a>-220, as one of its <a href="/wiki/Decay_products" title="Decay products" class="mw-redirect">decay products</a>. Secondary decay products of thorium include <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Actinium" title="Actinium">actinium</a>. Because of this, there are concerns about the safety of thorium mantles. Some <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety" title="Nuclear safety" class="mw-redirect">nuclear safety</a> agencies make recommendations about their use.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> Production of gas mantles has led to some safety concerns during <a href="/wiki/Gas_mantle#Safety_concerns" title="Gas mantle">manufacture</a>.</p> 
     <p>The chemical toxicity of thorium is low because thorium and its most common compounds (mostly the dioxide) are poorly soluble in water.<sup id="cite_ref-Schneckenstein_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schneckenstein-119"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> Nevertheless, some thorium compounds are chemically moderately <a href="/wiki/Toxic" title="Toxic" class="mw-redirect">toxic</a>. People who work with thorium compounds are at a risk of <a href="/wiki/Dermatitis" title="Dermatitis">dermatitis</a>. It can take as much as thirty years after the ingestion of thorium for symptoms to manifest themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-Nature.27s_Building_Blocks_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nature.27s_Building_Blocks-114"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: lower-alpha;"> 
      <ol class="references"> 
       <li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Trace_radioisotope" title="Trace radioisotope">Traces</a> of primordial <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>-244 still exist in nature,<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> but this does not occur in quantity, unlike thorium and uranium. Furthermore, while <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a> is very slightly radioactive, its half-life is so long that its decay is negligible even over geological timespans.</span></li> 
       <li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The transition temperature is between 1.35&nbsp;and&nbsp;1.40&nbsp;K.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li> 
       <li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">[Rn]6d<sup>2</sup> is a very low-lying excited state configuration of Th<sup>2+</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder5960_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder5960-19"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li> 
       <li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><sup>232</sup>Th is in fact the shortest-lived nuclide that still has a half-life longer than the generally accepted age of the universe. It is the sixth-most unstable primordial nuclide: among the primordial nuclides, only <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238"><sup>238</sup>U</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium-40" title="Potassium-40"><sup>40</sup>K</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235"><sup>235</sup>U</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samarium-146" title="Samarium-146" class="mw-redirect"><sup>146</sup>Sm</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-244" title="Plutonium-244"><sup>244</sup>Pu</a> have shorter half-lives.<sup id="cite_ref-NUBASE_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-24"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li> 
       <li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><sup>232</sup>Th has the following extremely rare decay modes: <a href="/wiki/Double_beta_decay" title="Double beta decay">double beta decay</a> to <a href="/wiki/Uranium-232" title="Uranium-232"><sup>232</sup>U</a>; <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_fission" title="Spontaneous fission">spontaneous fission</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Cluster_decay" title="Cluster decay">cluster decay</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ytterbium" title="Ytterbium"><sup>182</sup>Yb</a>, expelling <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon"><sup>26</sup>Ne</a> and <sup>24</sup>Ne clusters.<sup id="cite_ref-NUBASE_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-24"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li> 
       <li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The longest-lived intermediate decay product of <sup>232</sup>Th is its immediate alpha daughter <sup>228</sup>Ra, which has a half-life of only 5.7&nbsp;years.<sup id="cite_ref-NUBASE_24-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-24"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li> 
       <li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Technically, the 4<i>n</i> chain starts at primordial <sup>244</sup>Pu, which decays successively to <sup>240</sup>U, <sup>240</sup>Np, <sup>240</sup>Pu, <sup>236</sup>U, and then <sup>232</sup>Th: however, the concentration of primordial <sup>244</sup>Pu is now reduced to mere traces and hence these radioisotopes are <a href="/wiki/Extinct_radionuclide" title="Extinct radionuclide">extinct</a> for all practical purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-NUBASE_24-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-24"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li> 
       <li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This is shown by the fact that the rare earths are there given <a href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" title="Atomic weight" class="mw-redirect">atomic weight</a> values two-thirds of their actual ones, and thorium and uranium are given values half of their actual ones.</span></li> 
       <li id="cite_note-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-54">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A second extra-long periodic table row, to accommodate known and undiscovered elements with an atomic weight greater than bismuth (thorium, protactinium and uranium, for example), had been postulated as far back as 1892. Most investigators, however, considered that these elements were analogous to the third-row transition elements hafnium, <a href="/wiki/Tantalum" title="Tantalum">tantalum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a>. The existence of a second inner transition series, in the form of the actinides, was not accepted until similarities with the electron structures of the lanthanides had been established.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li> 
       <li id="cite_note-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-67">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The same also applies to uranium minerals, and many thorium minerals also contain uranium.<sup id="cite_ref-Woodhead_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woodhead-65"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ekanite_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ekanite-66"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li> 
       <li id="cite_note-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-68">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Thorianite refers to minerals with 75–100&nbsp;mol%&nbsp;ThO<sub>2</sub>; uranothorianite, 25–75&nbsp;mol%&nbsp;ThO<sub>2</sub>; thorian uraninite, 15–25&nbsp;mol%&nbsp;ThO<sub>2</sub>; <a href="/wiki/Uraninite" title="Uraninite">uraninite</a>, 0–15&nbsp;mol%&nbsp;ThO<sub>2</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder556_62-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder556-62"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li> 
       <li id="cite_note-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-69">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">More specifically, thorite refers to thorium silicate with the <a href="/wiki/Tetragonal_crystal_system" title="Tetragonal crystal system">tetragonal</a> <a href="/wiki/Zircon" title="Zircon">zircon</a> structure; <a href="/wiki/Monoclinic" title="Monoclinic" class="mw-redirect">monoclinic</a> thorium silicate is referred to as huttonite.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder556_62-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder556-62"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li> 
       <li id="cite_note-70"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-70">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">Hydroxide</a> ions may also replace silicate ions to form Th(SiO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>1−<i>x</i></sub>(OH)<sub>4<i>x</i></sub>, which as a mineral is named <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorogummite&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Thorogummite (page does not exist)">thorogummite</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder556_62-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder556-62"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li> 
       <li id="cite_note-83"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-83">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Among the low number of other known thorium oxometallates are the <a href="/wiki/Arsenate" title="Arsenate">arsenate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tungstate" title="Tungstate">tungstate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germanate" title="Germanate">germanate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Borate" title="Borate">borate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Perrhenate" title="Perrhenate">perrhenate</a>. While thorium <a href="/wiki/Titanate" title="Titanate">titanates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tantalate" title="Tantalate">tantalates</a> are known, they are structurally more like double oxides than true oxometallates.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickleder101115_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickleder101115-78"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li> 
       <li id="cite_note-88"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-88">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The thirteen fissile actinide isotopes with half-lives over a year are <sup>229</sup>Th, <sup>233</sup>U, <sup>235</sup>U, <a href="/wiki/Neptunium-236" title="Neptunium-236" class="mw-redirect"><sup>236</sup>Np</a>, <sup>239</sup>Pu, <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-241" title="Plutonium-241"><sup>241</sup>Pu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Americium-242m" title="Americium-242m" class="mw-redirect"><sup>242m</sup>Am</a>, <a href="/wiki/Curium-243" title="Curium-243" class="mw-redirect"><sup>243</sup>Cm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Curium-245" title="Curium-245" class="mw-redirect"><sup>245</sup>Cm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Curium-247" title="Curium-247" class="mw-redirect"><sup>247</sup>Cm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Californium-249" title="Californium-249" class="mw-redirect"><sup>249</sup>Cf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Californium-251" title="Californium-251" class="mw-redirect"><sup>251</sup>Cf</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Einsteinium-252" title="Einsteinium-252" class="mw-redirect"><sup>252</sup>Es</a>. Of these, only <sup>235</sup>U is naturally occurring, and only <sup>233</sup>U and <sup>239</sup>Pu can be bred from naturally occurring nuclei with single neutron capture.<sup id="cite_ref-Ronen_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ronen-86"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-anucene_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anucene-87"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li> 
      </ol> 
     </div> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;"> 
      <ol class="references"> 
       <li id="cite_note-IUPAC-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-IUPAC_1-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.ciaaw.org/atomic-weights.htm">Standard Atomic Weights 2013</a>. <a href="/wiki/Commission_on_Isotopic_Abundances_and_Atomic_Weights" title="Commission on Isotopic Abundances and Atomic Weights">Commission on Isotopic Abundances and Atomic Weights</a></span></li> 
       <li id="cite_note-magnet-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-magnet_2-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Lide, D. R., ed. (2005).
